1. Overview

Commonsense reasoning is the ordinary form of inference that lets people act sensibly in everyday life without rebuilding every conclusion from first principles. It includes expectations about objects, events, causes, goals, habits, social interaction, and the likely continuation of familiar situations. A person does not normally stop to prove that a cup dropped on a hard floor may break, that a closed door blocks passage, or that a conversation depends on shared context. These judgments arise quickly because commonsense reasoning compresses experience into ready-to-use expectations. It turns repeated experience into practical intelligence, and it does so at a pace that is fast enough for action.

In artificial intelligence, commonsense reasoning is not decorative. It is one of the clearest tests of whether a system understands the world as an organized environment of objects, events, intentions, and consequences rather than as disconnected symbols. The subject matters because everyday life is full of situations in which exact deductive certainty is unavailable but action still must proceed. A system that cannot reason commonsensically may still classify text or predict patterns, but it will struggle to interpret situations the way humans do. That makes commonsense reasoning central to philosophy of mind, knowledge representation, natural language understanding, planning, and human-like intelligence.

The topic also exposes a mismatch between formal logic and lived experience. Formal systems favor explicit premises, fixed rules, and stable inference. Everyday reasoning relies on background knowledge, flexibility, defaults, and exceptions. Commonsense reasoning sits at that intersection. It asks how to make informal knowledge precise enough for machines while keeping the flexibility that makes it useful for people. The challenge is not merely to store facts. It is to represent the world in a way that supports practical inference under uncertainty, change, ambiguity, and limited time. That combination gives the subject its enduring importance and explains why the literature keeps returning to it from different angles.

At the deepest level, commonsense reasoning is about how intelligence stays grounded. It is the background layer that makes interpretation possible, the hidden scaffolding that lets experience become expectation. Without it, facts remain isolated. With it, facts become usable. That is why researchers keep returning to the topic even as methods change from symbolic AI to statistical models and back again. The core question remains the same: how does a reasoning system know what is usually true, what is relevant now, and when an ordinary assumption should be abandoned? That question is still active because it sits at the boundary between useful pattern recognition and genuine understanding.

2. What Commonsense Means

Commonsense is the broad body of ordinary assumptions people use without consciously checking them. It includes expectations about objects, time, motion, social roles, communication, goals, causes, and the normal behavior of familiar situations. When a person hears that someone “went to the office,” they automatically infer that the person likely traveled, arrived, entered, and took up an appropriate role in that setting. These steps are not usually spelled out because they are part of the expected structure of the world. Commonsense therefore operates below deliberate reflection, yet it guides that reflection all the time.

This makes commonsense very different from strict mathematical proof. In mathematics, a conclusion is expected to follow necessarily from the premises. In ordinary life, a conclusion is often only plausible, provisional, and subject to correction. If someone sees wet pavement, they may infer that it rained, but that inference can be defeated by new evidence such as a street cleaner or a broken pipe. Commonsense is therefore rational without being absolute. It is practical rather than final. Its value comes from being useful in the normal case, not from being exceptionless in every case.

The ordinary character of commonsense is also why it is hard to formalize. It is not usually presented as a set of explicit rules. Instead, it is distributed across habit, memory, culture, background knowledge, and learned expectations. A human reasoner can use it quickly because it is deeply familiar. A formal system must reconstruct that familiarity in a more rigid medium. The challenge is to preserve the speed and flexibility of commonsense while giving it a representation that can support inference, explanation, and revision. That is why the topic continues to attract philosophers, logicians, linguists, and AI researchers alike.

Commonsense also has a social dimension. Much of what people know is not just about physical objects but about what is appropriate, likely, polite, relevant, or intentional in a given situation. This means commonsense cannot be reduced to a simple inventory of facts. It includes shared norms about behavior and interpretation. A commonsense system must therefore know not only what the world contains, but how situations usually unfold and how people usually respond. That combination of physical, social, and practical knowledge is what makes the subject so rich and why it remains resistant to a single neat formalization.

3. Everyday Assumptions

Much commonsense reasoning depends on assumptions that are so ordinary they are almost invisible. People assume floors will support them, cups will stay where placed, messages are meant to be interpreted, and doors behave in familiar ways. These assumptions save enormous effort because they allow attention to focus on what is unusual rather than on verifying every stable background condition. Everyday life would become impossible if each small action required a fresh proof that the surrounding world still behaves normally.

For AI, this creates a major challenge. A system may store many facts, but if it cannot organize them into ordinary expectations, it still cannot reason commonsensically. It needs a way to infer what is likely true in a familiar setting and to identify which facts are probably irrelevant. A person looking at a table does not need to prove that the table still exists while they look away from it. They simply assume persistence unless something indicates a change. That kind of effortless stability is one of the hallmarks of human intelligence, and one of the hardest things to reproduce formally. The reason is not that the assumption is complex, but that it is so pervasive and so deeply embedded in action.

Everyday assumptions are also deeply contextual. A hand on a door handle may signal an intent to open the door, but the same posture could mean someone is steadying themselves or demonstrating the handle for another person. Commonsense is therefore not just a set of unconditional assumptions. It is a disciplined habit of guessing what matters in a situation, based on what normally matters in similar situations. That is why background knowledge and context are inseparable from commonsense reasoning. What feels obvious to a human usually depends on many invisible layers of experience working together.

When formal systems fail in this area, the failure is often not in the facts themselves but in the missing background structure that makes the facts meaningful. The same raw statement can lead to very different conclusions depending on what a system silently assumes about the world. That silent layer is the true target of commonsense modeling. It is what makes human reasoning efficient, economical, and often surprisingly accurate in ordinary life. It is also what makes the field hard: the better the system performs, the more the background assumptions disappear into the success.

4. Defaults

Commonsense reasoning is full of defaults. Birds normally fly, glasses normally hold liquid, messages normally have intended meaning, and people normally follow conversation patterns. A default is not a universal law. It is a conclusion that holds in the normal case and can be overridden by further information. Defaults are valuable because they package a large amount of ordinary expectation into a compact form. They allow reasoning to proceed quickly without needing to inspect every possible exception in advance.

Defaults are essential because a reasoning system that could only act after complete certainty would rarely be useful. Human beings do not wait for total information before interpreting a situation or taking action. They move forward on the basis of what is typical and then revise if needed. Commonsense reasoning mirrors that pattern. It relies on what is usually true, not because ordinary truth is perfect, but because ordinary truth is usually sufficient for action. That is what makes defaults central to practical intelligence. They are a form of economical commitment, not blind certainty.

In formal AI, defaults have been studied to explain how one can infer normal properties without making brittle assumptions. This is important because a rule that looks reasonable can fail when the world contains exceptions. A default system must therefore support fallback behavior. It must allow the ordinary inference while keeping the possibility of revision open. This balance between speed and openness is one of the most important characteristics of commonsense reasoning. It is also one of the strongest reasons why non-monotonic and defeasible frameworks became so influential. These frameworks recognize that ordinary life is not a world of all-or-nothing conclusions.

Defaults also reveal something about human cognition itself. Much of thought is not a long chain of strict deductions. It is the rapid application of patterns learned from experience. That is why commonsense reasoning feels natural and immediate. It does not derive every conclusion from scratch. It leans on defaults that have been reinforced by repeated encounters with the world. The challenge for AI is to capture that same efficiency without losing control over correctness. A useful default system must therefore be both fast enough for practical use and restrained enough to admit correction.

5. Exceptions

Every commonsense rule has exceptions. Birds normally fly, except when injured, young, or of a species that does not. Wet roads usually mean rain, except when the road has been washed or a pipe has burst. Cars normally start when the ignition works, except when the battery is dead or fuel is absent. Exception sensitivity is not a weakness in commonsense reasoning. It is what makes commonsense accurate. The world is not made of rigid universal patterns alone; it is made of patterns with boundaries, conditions, and special cases.

Exceptions force a reasoning system to avoid overcommitting to a generalization. A statement like “all birds fly” is too strong for everyday intelligence. Humans know that generality must be held lightly. They use a rule when it is useful, but they also recognize the possibility of defeat. This is why commonsense is deeply connected to defeasible reasoning. A conclusion may be good enough in the ordinary case and still give way when a more specific fact appears. That flexibility is central to natural judgment and to the way people navigate the world without constant surprise.

The existence of exceptions also explains why commonsense systems cannot simply collect more and more rules without structure. A larger rule base can become brittle if it lacks a mechanism for revision, priority, and context. Human reasoners often resolve this through layered knowledge: broad expectations first, then more specific information if available. AI systems need comparable organization. Otherwise they become trapped between two failures: saying too little because they are cautious, or saying too much because they treat defaults as absolutes. In practice, a commonsense model must know how to step aside when the exception is the real story.

Exception handling is one of the clearest measures of whether a commonsense system actually understands a domain. It is easy to match the normal case. It is much harder to behave sensibly when the normal case is interrupted. That is why exceptions are not peripheral. They are a defining feature of the subject. A good commonsense model must know what usually happens and also know when usual behavior should be suspended. This is precisely where many systems fail in ways that are obvious to people but difficult to diagnose mechanically.

6. Defeasibility

Defeasible reasoning is reasoning that is rationally justified but not final. The conclusion can be supported by the available evidence and still be defeated later by a better fact, a more specific rule, or a changed context. This is one of the most natural features of ordinary reasoning. People constantly make tentative conclusions: a sound in the hall may suggest someone arrived, a closed shop may suggest it is late, a red sky may suggest weather change. Such inferences are useful because they are quick, but they remain open to correction.

Defeasibility matters because everyday reasoning is rarely complete. Human beings operate in environments where information is partial, noisy, or evolving. If a reasoning system waited for perfect confirmation every time, it would be too slow to function in the real world. Defeasible reasoning gives a way to act now and revise later. This makes it especially appropriate for practical intelligence, where the goal is not absolute proof but timely, reliable judgment under uncertainty. In other words, defeasibility is not a defect in reasoning; it is a structural feature of reasoning in a changing world.

In AI, defeasible reasoning is important because many tasks depend on incomplete evidence. A system interpreting a scene, a conversation, or a plan must often infer the most likely explanation before all facts are known. That inference should be usable but not irrevocable. Defeasibility provides exactly that structure. It allows a model to encode typical behavior while preserving the possibility that a later observation will change the conclusion. This is the logic of ordinary adaptation. It is especially useful when the environment is open-ended and not fully enumerable in advance.

Defeasible reasoning also mirrors human learning. People do not merely collect conclusions; they adjust the strength of their expectations as experience accumulates. The world teaches through repeated correction. Commonsense reasoning is one of the ways that correction becomes usable. It lets intelligence remain robust without becoming rigid. That is why defeasibility is one of the core concepts in the study of commonsense inference and one of the bridges between philosophy and operational AI systems.

7. Non-Monotonicity

Classical deduction is monotonic: once a conclusion follows, adding more premises does not invalidate it. Commonsense reasoning is often non-monotonic: new information can defeat a previous conclusion. A person may infer that a house is occupied when lights are on, but later learn that the lights were left on automatically. The later fact does not make the earlier inference irrational; it simply shows that the earlier inference was provisional. Real intelligence must be able to revise itself in response to new evidence.

Non-monotonic logic was developed to model this kind of revisable inference. It allows a reasoning system to hold defaults and then retract them when necessary. This is essential for commonsense because ordinary situations are not static. New facts appear. Hidden facts are revealed. Context changes. An intelligent agent must therefore be able to update belief without collapsing into inconsistency or stubbornness. Non-monotonic frameworks are designed to capture that dynamic behavior formally, and they do so by giving revision a place in the logic rather than treating revision as an afterthought.

The significance of non-monotonicity goes beyond technical logic. It represents a deeper view of intelligence as adaptive rather than merely accumulative. A reasoning system does not become smarter simply by preserving every conclusion forever. Sometimes the most intelligent act is to retract a belief in light of better information. Commonsense reasoning is built on that ability. It is not a one-way path from premises to conclusion. It is a living process of tentative commitment, review, and correction. That is what keeps it aligned with the world instead of with stale inference history.

This is one reason non-monotonic logic remains central in AI, planning, diagnosis, and knowledge representation. It matches the structure of uncertain environments more closely than purely monotonic frameworks do. Human reasoning is not weakened by revision; it is strengthened by it. A commonsense system must therefore be designed to treat revision as a feature, not a flaw. That design principle appears repeatedly in the literature because it captures something fundamental about intelligent behavior in practice.

8. Background Knowledge

Commonsense depends on a dense web of background knowledge that is usually implicit. People do not consciously store a separate rule for every ordinary action. Instead, they carry a broad sense of how objects, events, and people usually behave. This background is what makes immediate understanding possible. When someone says “the glass fell,” the listener does not need an explanation of gravity, fragility, or collision. Those ideas are already available in the background model of the world.

The challenge for AI is not merely to collect facts, but to organize them so that relevant background can be activated when needed. A large knowledge base without structure is not enough. The system must know which facts are pertinent, which are ordinary, and which are likely to override others. Background knowledge is therefore not just storage; it is an inference resource. It shapes the system’s ability to interpret, predict, and explain ordinary situations. This is why older symbolic approaches and newer neural systems both return to the same issue: knowing something is not the same as making it usable in context.

Background knowledge also includes many things people never articulate explicitly. It includes assumptions about timing, purpose, physical stability, social roles, and conversational relevance. These are often so deeply ingrained that they only become visible when a misunderstanding occurs. That invisibility is part of their power. The less attention they require, the more efficiently reasoning proceeds. Commonsense reasoning is thus built on a hidden architecture of expectations that support immediate interpretation. When this architecture is missing, even small scenes can feel baffling to a machine.

When background knowledge is absent, even simple scenes can become difficult for an AI system. The system may see a sequence of events but fail to know why they matter or what they imply. That is why background knowledge is one of the central foundations of commonsense AI. It is the difference between isolated recognition and situated understanding. It is also the reason many benchmarks and surveys focus on knowledge acquisition: without usable background, the system may be fluent but still not grounded.

9. The Frame Problem

The frame problem is the difficulty of representing change without having to list every irrelevant non-change explicitly. If someone moves a book from one table to another, most of the world remains unchanged. Human beings automatically assume that unmentioned features persist unless there is reason to think otherwise. A formal system, however, can struggle to represent this persistence compactly. The burden of specifying what does not change is what makes the frame problem so influential in AI and philosophy.

The problem matters because commonsense action reasoning must be selective. An intelligent agent cannot recalculate the entire state of the world after every event. It needs a concise way to represent effects while leaving everything else stable by default. The frame problem therefore exposes a deep tension between explicit representation and practical efficiency. The more detailed the encoding, the harder it may be to use; the more compact the encoding, the more difficult it may be to justify persistence. That tension has shaped much of the research on action and change, and it continues to appear in modern systems that must manage state efficiently.

Human beings solve the frame problem informally by relying on ordinary expectations. They know that most properties are unaffected by most actions. They infer that moving a cup does not alter the color of the wall unless something specific suggests otherwise. This sort of selective attention is one of the reasons human reasoning is so efficient. The challenge for AI is to obtain the same selectivity without losing formal control. That is why the frame problem remains a classic test of commonsense modeling. It is a litmus test for whether a system can track change without drowning in irrelevant detail.

It also shows why commonsense reasoning is inseparable from representation. If a system cannot express inertia, persistence, and selective change elegantly, it will pay a heavy cost in complexity. Many later formalisms were motivated by this challenge. The frame problem is therefore not just a technical inconvenience. It is a signpost pointing toward the need for better models of ordinary action, default persistence, and local change. It is one of the clearest places where philosophical questions and engineering constraints meet directly.

10. The Logic of Action

The logic of action studies how formal systems represent actions, plans, consequences, and agents. It is closely linked to commonsense reasoning because ordinary life is full of actions that alter the world in predictable but limited ways. People open doors, move objects, send messages, and make choices. Each of these actions changes some things while leaving most things unchanged. Commonsense reasoning must therefore track not only states of affairs but also the transformations produced by action.

Actions are difficult to model because they have intended effects, side effects, and context-sensitive outcomes. Turning on a lamp may illuminate a room, but it may also wake a sleeping person or reveal something previously hidden. A formal model of action must capture what normally follows from an action without collapsing into a long list of special cases. That is why the logic of action is so closely tied to the frame problem. It must explain change in a way that preserves the stability of everything not directly affected, and it must do so in a way that remains computationally manageable.

In commonsense reasoning, action is central because many everyday inferences are really predictions about consequences. If someone reaches for an umbrella, one may infer that rain is expected. If someone closes a window, one may infer concern about weather, noise, or privacy. Such inferences are not merely about motion; they are about goals and practical situations. The logic of action provides a framework for representing this connection between intention, event, and consequence. It also shows why commonsense is inseparable from practical agency rather than being a detached theory of facts.

This makes action theory one of the key bridges between static knowledge and dynamic intelligence. A commonsense system that understands action can reason about planning, causation, and change. One that cannot will remain limited to passive description. That is why the logic of action is a foundational component of formal commonsense reasoning. It is also why action reasoning remains relevant across robotics, planning, dialogue, and everyday world modeling.

11. Scripts and Routines

Humans often reason with scripts: familiar sequences for restaurants, classrooms, airports, meetings, and many other ordinary situations. A script is not a strict rule. It is a typical pattern that helps people predict what usually comes next and what roles are involved. If someone says they went to a restaurant, listeners immediately anticipate ordering, receiving food, eating, and paying, even if not every step is mentioned. Scripts are one of the main ways commonsense becomes efficient. They convert recurring life patterns into ready-made expectations.

Scripts compress ordinary experience into a reusable structure. They reduce the effort needed to understand a new instance of a familiar setting because the person can infer the missing steps from the general pattern. This is one reason dialogue and narrative are so dependent on commonsense. Speakers often omit what they assume is already known. Listeners fill in the omitted material using script-like expectations. The result is smooth communication without constant elaboration. The same mechanism helps people understand a story, follow instructions, or anticipate the shape of a social event.

For AI, scripts are both useful and dangerous. They help systems predict likely sequences, but they can also lead to brittle behavior if treated too rigidly. Real situations often deviate from the expected routine in subtle ways. A restaurant may be self-service, a meeting may be remote, or an airport may have unusual procedures. A commonsense system must therefore know not only the routine but also how to adapt when the routine breaks. That is what makes script knowledge a practical challenge. Scripts are a guide, not a guarantee.

Scripts are also closely related to social knowledge. Many ordinary routines involve not just actions but expectations about people and roles. That means a commonsense model must understand interaction as well as sequence. Scripts show how ordinary reasoning works not by proving each step, but by anticipating the shape of the situation. They are one of the clearest examples of how humans use background knowledge to make the world legible, and they remain a useful model for conversational and narrative AI.

12. Categories and Prototypes

Commonsense reasoning uses categories in a prototype-like way. People understand what a chair, fruit, tool, or vehicle is not only through formal definitions but through typical examples and familiar features. A prototype is a summary of what is normal for a category. It is not an exact logical definition, yet it supports quick inference. If something is recognized as a bird, a person may infer that it probably flies, sings, or nests, even though not every bird does all of these things.

Prototype-based thinking is useful because it makes category recognition efficient. Humans do not need to consult a full definition before acting on a category judgment. They rely on what is typical. That allows fast everyday reasoning. But prototypes also bring risk. If a system overcommits to the typical case, it may fail to handle unusual members of the category. A penguin is still a bird even though it violates the prototype of bird behavior. Commonsense therefore needs both typicality and exception management. This is a recurring lesson across classification, memory, and inference.

In AI, category structure is important because it determines what properties are inherited and what expectations are activated. A system that understands a thing as a vehicle may infer motion, transport, and capacity. But it should also remain open to different subtypes, special conditions, and edge cases. This is one reason commonsense representation often involves layered classification rather than rigid definitions. Real-world categories are rarely perfectly bounded. They overlap, vary by context, and support graded membership. A good commonsense model must therefore treat category knowledge as usable but not final.

The prototype view also helps explain why commonsense reasoning is often graded rather than absolute. Some examples are more central than others. Some inferences are more natural than others. Human reasoning uses those degrees of typicality constantly. That makes prototype-like categories one of the most practical and philosophically interesting parts of commonsense knowledge. It also explains why benchmarks that force single correct answers can miss much of the nuance in ordinary conceptual understanding.

13. Analogy

Analogical reasoning is one of the most important tools of commonsense. People understand a new situation by comparing it with something familiar. If a device behaves like a locked door, a similar opening action may be needed. If a conversation resembles a negotiation, a similar pattern of offers and responses may apply. Analogy works because it transfers structure from a known case to a new one. This makes it one of the fastest and most flexible ways to extend understanding.

Analogies are powerful because they compress reasoning. Instead of deriving every detail from scratch, a person can map a known pattern onto a fresh situation and then refine the mapping as needed. This makes analogy valuable in explanation, learning, and problem solving. It is often the bridge between unfamiliarity and practical action. A commonsense reasoner who can analogize can handle novelty better than one who relies only on fixed rules. Analogy is also central to many human explanations because it helps a listener see a new problem through the frame of an old one.

The danger is overextension. Similarity in one respect does not guarantee similarity in all respects. A good analogy is useful precisely because it captures the right structure, not merely because two things look alike. Commonsense reasoning therefore uses analogy as a guide rather than a proof. The system must be able to ask which aspects are genuinely shared and which are not. That is where background knowledge and context become essential. The right comparison must be selected, not merely noticed.

Analogy also matters because human communication often depends on it. People explain unfamiliar concepts by comparison to familiar ones. That means analogical thinking is not only a reasoning tool but a communication tool. In commonsense reasoning, it helps connect the new to the known. That is why analogy remains a central concept in both philosophy and AI studies of everyday intelligence. It is especially valuable when a system must explain itself in human terms rather than in internal machine categories.

14. Context

Commonsense reasoning is deeply context-sensitive. The same statement, object, or action can mean different things in different settings. A cold room may refer to temperature, atmosphere, or social tone. A person standing at a podium may be giving a lecture, making an announcement, or waiting nervously. Context determines which interpretation is appropriate. Without context, general rules are too blunt to be useful.

Context matters because commonsense is not just about the world in abstraction. It is about the world as situated in a particular moment, goal, and setting. A system must know which background assumptions are active right now. That means it must identify what is relevant and what can be safely ignored. This selective activation is one of the most human-like features of everyday reasoning. It is also one of the most difficult to formalize, because relevance depends on both the situation and the goal. In practice, the same fact can be crucial in one context and irrelevant in another.

For AI, context management is often the difference between plausible and appropriate reasoning. A system may know many facts but still choose the wrong one if it cannot see the current frame. Commonsense therefore depends on the ability to interpret local conditions rather than applying general rules mechanically. Context lets the same knowledge support different conclusions in different circumstances. That flexibility is central to real intelligence. It is also what enables ordinary dialogue, where people rely on shared situational knowledge to compress what they say.

In natural language, context is especially important because people omit information that they assume is obvious. Listeners fill the gaps by using the surrounding situation. Commonsense reasoning is what allows that filling-in to happen reliably. That is why context is not an accessory to commonsense. It is one of its basic operating conditions. Without it, even correct facts can become misapplied, and correct inferences can sound absurd.

15. Ambiguity

Commonsense reasoning helps resolve ambiguity by filling in likely meanings. When language or behavior is underdetermined, people infer the most plausible interpretation from surrounding cues. This is not random guessing. It is structured expectation based on prior experience. A sentence like “she saw the man with the telescope” can support multiple readings, but ordinary listeners quickly choose one based on plausibility and context. That quick resolution is one of the quiet achievements of commonsense cognition.

Ambiguity is important because the world rarely presents itself in perfectly explicit form. Human beings operate with incomplete sentences, incomplete scenes, and incomplete intentions. Commonsense reasoning allows them to infer what is most likely meant even when the signal is imperfect. That is one reason language understanding and commonsense belong together. Interpreting meaning often requires filling in what is not said directly. It also requires judging which interpretation best fits the broader situation rather than the bare text alone.

For AI, ambiguity is a difficult problem because many inputs support more than one interpretation. A system without commonsense may treat all readings equally and fail to prioritize the most natural one. A system with commonsense can rank interpretations by plausibility. This ranking is not deductive certainty; it is defeasible judgment. That is exactly the kind of inference that everyday intelligence performs constantly. Ambiguity therefore becomes a testing ground for commonsense in both language and action domains, and it exposes how much interpretation depends on background expectations.

The ability to resolve ambiguity is also a sign of efficient cognition. A person does not need to consider every possible meaning equally. They use background knowledge to narrow the field quickly. That selectivity is what makes everyday understanding possible. Commonsense reasoning is thus one of the main engines of ambiguity resolution in human life. It is also why many benchmark items appear easy to humans yet remain difficult for systems that lack rich world models.

16. Relevance and Salience

Commonsense reasoning is selective. It does not treat every fact as equally important. Some details are salient, while others are background noise. This selectivity is one of the hidden strengths of human cognition. People know what matters for the task at hand and can ignore what is irrelevant. Without that filtering, ordinary reasoning would be too slow and too expensive to be practical.

Relevance is difficult for formal systems because logic alone does not automatically separate useful information from irrelevant information. A system may know many true statements and still fail to identify which ones matter now. That is why commonsense AI must combine logical representation with prioritization and context management. The key issue is not simply whether a conclusion follows, but whether it follows from the right facts at the right level of detail. Relevance determines whether reasoning is useful rather than merely correct in the abstract. A proof or inference can be valid and still be the wrong move for the moment.

Salience also affects communication. In conversation, people highlight some facts and leave others unspoken because they assume the listener can infer the rest. A commonsense system must know which details to attend to and which to suppress. This makes salience a practical form of intelligence. It is not enough to know the world. A reasoning system must know which parts of the world are presently important. This is one reason conversational systems fail when they answer a side issue instead of the one that actually matters.

This is one reason commonsense reasoning is so central to dialogue systems, planning systems, and interactive agents. A system that cannot identify relevance will appear noisy, verbose, or oddly detached from the situation. Human-like reasoning depends on the ability to focus. Commonsense is one of the main mechanisms by which that focus is achieved. It is not merely a source of content; it is a discipline of attention.

17. Missing Information

Commonsense reasoning works even when information is incomplete. People often reach useful conclusions from partial clues, fragmentary observation, or imperfect memory. This is one of the main reasons commonsense matters in daily life: the world rarely provides all the information one would like. A person can still act sensibly by using what is likely and by keeping the result open to revision. That capacity is a central part of practical intelligence.

In AI, missing information is normal rather than exceptional. A system may not know every property of an object or every fact about a situation, yet it still needs to decide what to infer. The challenge is to avoid both paralysis and overconfidence. Commonsense reasoning offers a middle path: draw the best conclusion available, remain open to correction, and revise when new evidence arrives. That is how humans move through uncertainty without being trapped by it. The point is not to be omniscient, but to be resilient under uncertainty.

Missing information also explains why non-monotonic and defeasible logic are so important. A system must be able to operate with only partial evidence. If a later fact arrives, the system should update gracefully. That is the logic of ordinary life. People do not stop acting because information is incomplete. They use the best available model and adjust when required. Commonsense is therefore a strategy for handling incompleteness rather than a guarantee against it. It is the art of not needing the full picture before taking the next sensible step.

Systems that ignore missing information may look strong in controlled examples but fail in open environments. Commonsense reasoning is valuable precisely because it helps bridge that gap. It turns partial knowledge into workable guidance. That is one of the reasons it remains such a difficult and important problem in AI. The challenge is not simply filling gaps, but deciding which gaps matter enough to slow belief, and which can safely be bridged by ordinary expectation.

18. Abduction

Abductive reasoning is inference to the best explanation. It is one of the most natural forms of commonsense because people constantly ask not only what is true, but why it is true. If the floor is wet, possible explanations include rain, a spill, or cleaning. Abduction does not guarantee truth, but it gives direction to inquiry and action. It helps transform a surprising observation into a manageable hypothesis.

Abduction is especially useful in diagnosis, interpretation, and prediction. It connects observations to likely causes in a way that pure deduction does not. In commonsense reasoning, this matters because people usually need an explanation before they can decide what to do next. They want the most plausible story that fits the available evidence. That story then guides further checking, communication, or response. Abduction therefore plays a central role in how ordinary intelligence moves from observation to understanding. It is the bridge from “something happened” to “here is what is probably going on.”

For AI, abduction is important because a system should not merely classify or predict; it should also explain. An explanation helps the system decide which assumptions are reasonable, which are weak, and what additional evidence would matter. This makes abductive reasoning a bridge between perception and action. It is one of the best ways to make commonsense reasoning productive rather than purely descriptive. A system that can generate a plausible explanation is often closer to practical use than one that merely outputs a label.

Abduction also shows why commonsense is not reducible to fixed rules. The best explanation may depend on context, background knowledge, and expectation. A good commonsense reasoner uses abduction to create a working model of what is happening and then improves that model as more information appears. That dynamic explanatory process is one of the clearest signs of ordinary intelligence. It is also why so many commonsense benchmarks are ultimately testing explanation under uncertainty rather than rote pattern matching.

19. Explanation

Commonsense reasoning is deeply explanatory. People want to know why things happened, not just that they happened. Explanation helps stabilize belief, guide action, and support communication. A fact without a reason often remains unsatisfactory, even if it is accepted as true. Commonsense therefore does not stop at classification or recognition. It seeks to connect events with causes, intentions, and ordinary patterns that make the situation intelligible.

In everyday life, explanation usually means linking a surprising event to a familiar pattern. A light stopped working because the bulb burned out, a meeting ended early because the participants were busy, or a message arrived late because of traffic or delay. These explanations are not merely answers; they are ways of organizing experience so that it remains manageable. They also reveal what kinds of future events are likely. That is why explanation and prediction are so tightly linked in commonsense reasoning. To explain well is often to predict better the next time a similar situation appears.

For AI, explanation is essential because systems that only produce outputs without reasons are difficult to trust, debug, or improve. A commonsense model should ideally identify the assumptions that support an inference and the conditions under which it would fail. That makes the system more transparent and more usable. It also aligns the system with human expectations, since people naturally ask for the reason behind an answer. A model that can explain its inference is much closer to practical intelligence than one that can only output a label. This is why explainability and commonsense are so often discussed together in current work.

Explanation also serves a social function. It allows communication between people who do not share the same full background knowledge. Commonsense reasoning therefore supports not only internal inference but shared understanding. This makes explanation one of the deepest links between reasoning, language, and everyday cooperation. It is not just an output of reasoning; it is one of the ways reasoning becomes publicly usable.

20. Prediction

Commonsense supports prediction by extending ordinary patterns into the immediate future. If someone walks toward a door with keys in hand, people may expect the door to be opened. If a pot is left on a stove, people may expect heat, boiling, or intervention. These predictions are not laws of nature. They are sensible expectations based on experience. Prediction is one of the most valuable uses of commonsense because it lets agents prepare before events fully unfold.

The usefulness of prediction lies in its practical role. A person crossing a street, entering a room, or joining a conversation constantly anticipates what is likely to happen next. That anticipation reduces surprise and makes action smoother. In AI, prediction is difficult because the system must handle both regularity and interruption. A commonsense predictor should continue the pattern when the situation remains normal, but it should also detect when the pattern is being blocked or redirected. That requires context, background knowledge, and revision. The point is not simply to continue a sequence; it is to continue it intelligently.

Prediction also depends on explanation. A system that can explain why something is likely to happen can also refine its prediction when conditions change. This is why commonsense inference is so dynamic: it predicts, observes, revises, and predicts again. That cycle is much closer to everyday intelligence than a single fixed output. It reflects the fact that the world is unfolding, not static. A good commonsense system therefore behaves less like a classifier and more like a participant in a moving environment.

In a practical sense, prediction is how commonsense becomes useful before the outcome is known. It prepares the agent for likely states of the world. That is why prediction is one of the central achievements of commonsense reasoning. It turns ordinary patterns into actionable foresight. It also helps explain why humans often seem to “just know” what comes next: they are not guessing from nothing, but extending a well-practiced model of the world.

21. Belief Revision

Belief revision is the process of updating what one takes to be true when new information arrives. Commonsense reasoning requires belief revision because its conclusions are provisional. If a person assumes a store is open and later sees a “closed” sign, the earlier belief must be adjusted. That change is not a failure of rationality. It is the sign of a system that responds to evidence rather than clinging to outdated assumptions.

In logic-based AI, revisability is one of the strongest motivations for non-monotonic frameworks. They allow systems to preserve useful inferences while retracting them when new facts make the earlier inference unreliable. This is exactly what commonsense agents need. They act on the best current understanding, then update that understanding as the situation changes. A rigid system that never revises will slowly drift away from reality. Belief revision keeps the model aligned with the world. It also keeps the system from treating yesterday’s convenience as today’s truth.

Belief revision also matters for learning. Human beings rarely receive complete information at once. They build understanding over time, correcting themselves along the way. Commonsense reasoning mirrors that developmental process. It is not a frozen repository of beliefs. It is an adaptive process that maintains coherence while changing. That is why revision is not an optional feature. It is the maintenance system for ordinary intelligence. Without revision, a system can accumulate error as easily as it accumulates knowledge.

A commonsense system that cannot revise will eventually become brittle. One that can revise intelligently becomes more trustworthy, because it treats error correction as part of reasoning. That makes belief revision one of the most important components of practical inference. It preserves both flexibility and accountability, and it explains why commonsense is often less about being right once than about staying right as conditions change.

22. Logic-Based AI

Logic-based AI is one of the historical homes of commonsense reasoning. The central idea is to encode knowledge in a formal representation language and then reason over it using logical inference. Commonsense became a major test case because it exposed the gap between neat deduction and the messy structure of everyday understanding. If AI could not reason about ordinary situations, then it would remain weak in the environments humans actually inhabit. The problem was never only formal elegance. It was whether machines could cope with the ordinary world.

The importance of this tradition is that it treats commonsense not as a vague intuition but as a formal challenge. That move remains influential today. Even when current systems are learned statistically, the same questions persist: how does a system know what is normal, what can change, what is relevant, and what should be inferred only tentatively? Logic-based AI gives a vocabulary for those questions and a framework for connecting them to action, explanation, and knowledge representation. It is especially useful because it makes assumptions visible rather than hidden inside a large parameter space.

Logic-based AI also matters because it provides explicit structures that can be inspected, debugged, and revised. A commonsense system built only on hidden weights or implicit patterns may be powerful but difficult to interpret. Logic-based formulations make reasoning transparent. They show the premises and the inferential path. That clarity is especially useful when the subject is ordinary belief, because ordinary belief is exactly where hidden assumptions are most dangerous. The older symbolic tradition therefore still shapes the questions asked of newer systems.

For that reason, logic-based AI remains relevant even in the era of language models. It does not compete only on performance; it competes on explainability, control, and explicit structure. Commonsense reasoning keeps logic-based AI alive as a foundational research program because it demands exactly the kind of transparent adaptation that logic is good at expressing. That is also why many hybrid systems try to combine symbolic structure with learned components rather than choosing one side only.

23. Knowledge Bases

Commonsense reasoning often depends on structured knowledge bases. These are collections of facts, relations, and rules that support inference. A good commonsense knowledge base must do more than store information. It must support inheritance, defaults, exceptions, and revisable conclusions. That makes design as important as content. A huge unorganized repository is less useful than a smaller system that knows how to connect and prioritize ideas. The structure determines whether knowledge can actually be used.

Commonsense knowledge is vast, but its usefulness depends on organization. A system with too little background cannot interpret ordinary situations. A system with too much unstructured background may become unmanageable. The challenge is to represent enough of the everyday world to enable reasoning while keeping the representation computationally tractable. That is one of the central reasons commonsense remains hard and why knowledge representation continues to be a foundational AI problem. Surveys of knowledge acquisition and reasoning emphasize that the question is not just “what to know,” but “how to represent and retrieve it.”

Knowledge bases also reveal a distinction between knowing facts and knowing how to use facts. A commonsense system needs more than a warehouse of information. It needs a scaffold for inference. It must know how to activate relevant knowledge in a given context and how to avoid drowning in irrelevant detail. This is why design choices in representation matter so much. The same factual content can be either useful or unusable depending on how it is structured. A well-designed knowledge base makes ordinary inference feel obvious; a poorly designed one merely stores trivia.

In practice, a knowledge base is successful when it makes ordinary inferences feel effortless. That is the goal of commonsense modeling: not merely to remember the world, but to make the world inferentially available. A good knowledge base turns scattered facts into practical understanding. It supports explanation, prediction, and revision rather than only retrieval. That is why knowledge-base engineering remains a core companion to commonsense research.

24. Expert Systems

Expert systems historically relied on rule-based reasoning to encode domain expertise, and commonsense knowledge was often needed to keep those rules from becoming too narrow. A system might know many technical facts but still fail if it cannot interpret ordinary conditions, user intent, or default assumptions. Commonsense knowledge helps bridge that gap by filling in what the formal rules do not say explicitly. It allows specialized reasoning to function inside an ordinary world.

This is one reason commonsense became a frontier problem in AI. Expert knowledge alone is not enough if the system cannot handle ordinary background assumptions. A practical intelligent system must combine specialized competence with a general grasp of everyday situations. That combination is what gives commonsense strategic importance. Without it, expertise remains brittle and local. With it, expertise becomes usable in the situations that matter most. The ordinary setting is often where specialist knowledge either succeeds gracefully or fails embarrassingly.

Commonsense also helps expert systems interpret exceptions. A rule may describe the ordinary case, but the system must know when the ordinary case does not apply. That requires background knowledge about how the world normally works. In this sense, commonsense is not opposed to expertise; it supports it. A strong expert system needs both domain-specific knowledge and ordinary-world interpretation. The more complex the domain, the more valuable this ordinary scaffolding becomes, because edge cases can appear in surprising places.

The lesson carries into modern AI as well. Specialized performance is not enough if the system cannot situate itself in the world. Commonsense reasoning provides that situating structure. It is the glue between expert rule and lived context. That is why the topic remained important long after the original era of expert systems. It is also why contemporary systems still need a common-sense layer, even when the core model is learned from data rather than hand-coded as rules.

25. Planning

Planning requires commonsense because plans operate in a world of ordinary consequences. If an agent wants to reach a goal, it must know what actions normally do, what they usually do not do, and what side effects might matter. The logic of action is therefore closely linked to planning: both ask how an agent can move from the current state to a desired one. Planning is not just selecting steps. It is selecting steps in a way that respects how the world ordinarily behaves.

Commonsense planning must also track relevance. An action may affect one object, one room, one person, or one schedule without affecting everything else. That selectivity is exactly what the frame problem highlights. The planner needs a way to represent change without exploding into an enormous list of unrelated facts. That is why planning is one of the most practical tests of commonsense reasoning. A plan that ignores ordinary effects is likely to fail in the real world. A good plan is not merely correct in principle; it is robust under the actual conditions in which it will be carried out.

In human life, planning is usually not exhaustive. People do not compute every possible outcome. They rely on ordinary expectations about what will happen if they act. They know, for example, that leaving early may avoid traffic, that carrying an umbrella may prepare for rain, or that calling ahead may save time. These expectations are commonsense in action. They are simple, but they shape effective behavior. They let humans reason at the level of meaningful choices rather than the level of all possible future branches.

For AI, commonsense planning requires a model of state, action, and consequence that is compact but not careless. It must represent enough of the world to support useful decisions while remaining responsive to exceptions and contingencies. That balance is difficult, but it is exactly what makes planning a major domain for commonsense reasoning research. Planning is where abstract knowledge becomes practical commitment, and that makes the quality of commonsense visible very quickly.

26. Natural Language

Commonsense reasoning is deeply connected to language understanding. People rely on background knowledge to interpret pronouns, implied actions, conversational expectations, and understated references. A sentence can be grammatically complete and still require commonsense to be understood correctly. That is because meaning is often carried by the world model behind the words, not just by the words themselves. Language and commonsense therefore belong together.

This connection is especially important in AI because many language tasks depend on unstated assumptions. A system may need to know what “it,” “there,” “after that,” or “the usual way” refers to in context. Commonsense helps resolve such references by linking expressions to broader world knowledge. Without that background, a system can sound fluent while missing the situation it is describing. That is one reason language models can produce convincing text yet still struggle with ordinary-world consistency. Fluency alone does not guarantee situated understanding.

Commonsense also matters for pragmatic interpretation. People do not speak only to state facts. They ask, hint, imply, request, soften, and negotiate. Understanding those moves requires more than syntax and lexical meaning. It requires a sense of what is typical in social interaction. That social layer is a major part of commonsense reasoning. It explains why the same sentence can function differently in different contexts. The literal words may stay the same while the social force changes completely.

For this reason, language understanding is one of the clearest arenas in which commonsense becomes visible. The system must go beyond literal parsing to situate words in a world of goals, expectations, and shared assumptions. That is what makes commonsense indispensable to natural language processing and dialogue systems. It also explains why many language benchmarks test situations that are easy to read but hard to ground.

27. Commonsense in Large Language Models

Recent survey work shows that commonsense reasoning is now a major issue in the study of pre-trained language models and large language models. The key question is whether such models truly reason commonsensically or mainly reproduce surface patterns that resemble commonsense answers. This question matters because commonsense is one of the most widely invoked standards for human-like intelligence. A system may generate fluent responses and still fail to handle ordinary situations reliably.

One reason the problem remains open is that commonsense is not a single skill. It includes physical, social, temporal, causal, and intentional knowledge. A model may perform well in one of these areas and weakly in another. Another reason is that evaluation is difficult. Performance on common benchmarks can look strong even when the underlying reasoning is fragile. That makes commonsense assessment a moving target and a continuing research challenge. Some tasks also admit many plausible answers, so a single numerical score can hide genuine uncertainty.

The difficulty is that language models often rely on patterns learned from text, which can overlap with commonsense without fully capturing it. Text may contain many ordinary inferences, but not all forms of everyday reasoning are equally represented in language. Some commonsense knowledge is grounded in action, perception, and interaction rather than in text alone. That means language models can appear competent while still failing on situations that require stronger world grounding or more robust revision. In practice, the model may be good at the shape of commonsense while weaker on the lived structure behind it.

For this reason, commonsense reasoning in large language models is not a solved problem. It is an active research area that links representation, evaluation, and grounding. The challenge is not just to generate plausible sentences. It is to support reliable everyday inference across changing contexts and exceptions. That is why the topic continues to draw attention from both NLP and broader AI research. The newest systems may improve the surface, but the underlying problem remains: how to connect pattern to understanding.

28. Commonsense Benchmarks

Benchmarking commonsense reasoning is difficult because ordinary knowledge is often ambiguous, context-sensitive, and culturally variable. Survey work on commonsense benchmarks shows that a large number of evaluation tasks have been created, yet many still leave important parts of commonsense untested. Benchmarks are useful because they force researchers to define the target. But they are limited because many commonsense judgments admit more than one plausible answer. A system can score well on a benchmark and still fail in an ordinary situation where nuance matters more than a forced choice.

That is why benchmark design is so important. Good evaluation must check not only accuracy, but robustness, exception handling, and consistency under variation. A multiple-choice format can be helpful for measurement, but it is also a simplification of the way humans actually reason. Real commonsense often depends on graded plausibility, context, and competing explanations rather than a single best answer isolated from the rest of the situation. This is one reason the literature increasingly criticizes benchmark items that look objective while hiding subjective or underdetermined judgments.

The breadth of benchmarking also shows that commonsense reasoning is not one problem but many. Some benchmarks test physical reasoning, some social reasoning, some causal prediction, and some narrative inference. That variety reflects the breadth of commonsense itself. It also reveals why measuring progress is difficult: success in one subset does not guarantee success in another. A commonsense benchmark must therefore be interpreted as a partial lens rather than a complete score of intelligence. A narrow test can reward the appearance of knowledge without fully testing the structure that makes the knowledge usable.

Benchmarks remain valuable because they create a common language for comparison. But they should be read carefully. The central question is not only whether a model answers correctly, but whether it does so for the right reasons and in the right situations. That caution is essential in a topic as context-sensitive as commonsense reasoning. The best benchmark is therefore one that exposes ambiguity rather than pretending it does not exist.

29. Conversational Commonsense

Commonsense reasoning is especially important in conversation because dialogue depends on shared assumptions, unstated references, and rapid interpretation of intent. A conversation tutorial on commonsense reasoning emphasizes physical and social commonsense, methods for gathering and representing commonsense knowledge, and ways to evaluate commonsense abilities in systems. Conversation is a severe test because speakers omit what they assume the listener can infer. The gap between literal wording and intended meaning must be filled in by background understanding.

Conversational AI therefore needs more than grammatical fluency. It needs to understand why an utterance is made, what background knowledge it presupposes, and what the likely next move should be. A model that can answer a question but misses the underlying situation will often respond in ways that are locally plausible yet globally off target. The reason is that conversation is not a sequence of isolated utterances. It is a coordinated social process with expectations about relevance, cooperation, and shared context. People respond to each other as participants in a scene, not as disconnected text generators.

Commonsense is also essential for dialogue because people speak indirectly. They hint rather than state, imply rather than spell out, and rely on the other person to fill in the gaps. That is impossible to handle well without ordinary-world reasoning. A conversational system that lacks commonsense may sound fluent but still miss the point of the exchange. That is why dialogue remains one of the clearest demonstrations of the practical importance of commonsense reasoning. It is where omission becomes normal and understanding has to be inferred at high speed.

In interactive systems, conversational commonsense is not just a nice extra. It is the core mechanism that allows a system to feel situated in the interaction. It must know what the conversation is about, what has already been established, and what would count as a reasonable next step. That makes conversation one of the most revealing tests of commonsense intelligence. It is also one of the most unforgiving, because bad context handling becomes obvious immediately to human users.

30. Final Synthesis

Commonsense reasoning is the everyday intelligence that makes formal systems usable in real environments. It is defeasible, non-monotonic, context-sensitive, and closely tied to action, explanation, analogy, and background knowledge. The philosophical literature on logic-based AI, defeasible reasoning, non-monotonic logic, the frame problem, and the logic of action explains the classical foundations of the subject. The survey and tutorial literature on commonsense knowledge, benchmarks, conversational AI, language models, and knowledge acquisition shows how those same issues continue to shape current research. The additional recent sources reinforce the same point from different angles: commonsense remains central because ordinary intelligence still depends on it.

The central lesson is that commonsense is not a decorative layer added after intelligence is already complete. It is the background structure that makes ordinary inference possible. Without it, a system cannot interpret context, handle exceptions, revise beliefs, or plan actions in a changing world. With it, reasoning becomes practical. That is why commonsense remains one of the most persistent problems in AI and one of the clearest windows into how human understanding actually works. It is the place where theory meets daily life.

Modern systems may be better than earlier systems at pattern recognition, generation, and retrieval, but the basic challenge remains: how does an intelligent system know what is normally true, when to trust that normality, and when to abandon it in light of a better explanation or a new fact? That question is still at the center of commonsense reasoning. It connects philosophy, AI, logic, linguistics, and cognition in a single shared problem. The field continues to evolve, but the core task remains unchanged: to make ordinary understanding explicit enough to reason with, while keeping it flexible enough to reflect the world as it really behaves. The enduring importance of the topic lies in that balance between structure and adaptability, between what is typical and what is true right now.
Intuition
